B00EO10M54|5.0|So, the world is talking about Miley Cyrus right now, so why shouldn't I?  My wife would probably prefer that I don't, since she kind of hates Cyrus and her degrading antics, but at the end of the day this girl is on fire right now so I can't resist.<br /><br />Many have been balking about Cyrus's more resent appearances, picking apart everything she says, attacking her on public forums (celebrities, or former celebrities, trying to latch onto her fame by writing open letters to her criticizing her flagrant `exposure'), talking about her VMA performance as if she were infecting the audience with disease.  She's become the `go-to' for every entertainment newscast, every radio station and every internet forum.  Since she released her drug addled music video for `We Can't Stop', it appears that the world has been hesitant to embrace her new image and have been intent on tarnishing her image by nitpicking her every move.<br /><br />Apparently, this girl is Hitler.<br /><br />I have to say this; Miley Cyrus knows what she is doing.  The world, love or hate, can't stop thinking about her, and at the end of the day, that sells records.  But really, what is a girl to do?  You grow up Disney, you live in the shadow of your parent's success and are constantly being linked to him and even criticized for the relation.  You want to shake this good girl image because you can't stay `Hannah Montana' forever.  If you do, you'll fall into obscurity.  You are too old to continue doing the show and snagging new fans, and your fan base is getting older and is going to find you pathetic before long.  Then you look at your former Disney girls like Selena Gomez and Demi Lovato.  Lovato has a hit show and has branched out to an older crowd thanks to her personal connection to them through her own struggles and rehab.  She's become a spokesperson of sorts.  You can't do that.  Gomez has reached a bigger audience thanks to her acting, and her recent edgy film `Spring Breakers' certainly played a big hand in her album sales.<br /><br />But you want more.  You have the Hollywood boyfriend and the money to go along with it, but you want to be KNOWN.<br /><br />The problem is that no matter how much talent you have (and this girl has TALENT), no one takes you seriously as you are.  No one cares.  That is where her current outlandish behavior pays off.  Up until she became a sex vixen, no one cared about this girl.  We all knew who she was, but no one over fifteen was going to buy her album, no matter how good her song on the radio was.  She could have released this very album and the sales would have been moderate at best and even the reviews wouldn't have encouraged the masses to take a bite.  Cyrus was VERY smart in jumping right out of the box and saying LOOK AT ME!<br /><br />We're looking.<br /><br />Besides, she prepared us for the drugs, sex and language in store for us.  Believe me, the first time you hear Cyrus drop the F-bomb is a different feeling than the twentieth time, but had she not given us all a taste of her newfound confidence and complete `exposure', it may have been uncomfortable to listen to.  She fits this new edgy persona like a glove (or I should say that it fits her) and this album, and her image, go hand in hand.<br /><br />When I say that this album is brilliant, I mean it.  I've been getting banged for an entire week, and I can't stop!<br /><br />`Bangerz', the album, is a wide range of wonderful.  From ballads to up tempo club heavy tracks, the album blends so many influences to the right amount to give us exactly what we want.  `Adore You' is the perfect introduction to the album, layering her mature vocals over a very laid back and sultry track.  Right from the start, Miley is telling us that she has grown up, but that this album isn't entirely about shaking her tail-feather.<br /><br />From there, Cyrus takes us to the club with her infectious `We Can't Stop', followed by the even more infectious `SMS (Bangerz)', which features Britney Spears.  I seriously have been singing this particular song for a week.  At random I find myself saying \\"effing Bangerz\\" and I couldn't be more happy about it.  The song is flirty, fun and riffs old school hip hop influences magically with Cyrus giving us her variation of rapping, and she sells it.  She struts her stuff rather well.  `4x4' is a brilliant way to fuse her club sound with her country roots, giving us a line dance on speed with a chant worthy chorus (you just WANT to sing along) and fun lyrics.<br /><br />And then she SLAYS us with two of the most beautifully composed ballads I've heard in a long time.  I've personally been in awe of the power of `Wrecking Ball' for a month now.  The song sends shivers down my spine, with beautifully time lyrics and a powerful chorus that commands.  What I didn't expect was the brilliant `My Darlin', which precedes it.  Enlisting Future to help her out, Cyrus paints a bleak picture of her fallen relationship (one can only assume she's talking about her fallout with Liam Hemsworth) and she adds so many layers to her new image and sound.  She falls into the island influences beautifully, developing a darker version of a Marley sound.  That chorus is killer.<br /><br />`Love Money Party' draws us right back into the party scene, with Cyrus rapping over bass heavy beats and running out of breath (in a good way) on that crazy chorus.  #GETITRIGHT leaves the club, or at least the part of the club where people are taking off their clothes, and gives us to a more sophisticated up tempo jam that flaunts her stellar vocals and gives homage to the jazzy funk of the 60's.<br /><br />Then she hits `Drive' and brings back the devastating with a ballad that crashes down on us with a chorus that gives me chills.  The way her voice pierces with every stab of her tongue, calling out Liam and serving up scorned realness.  She turns that scorned persona on its head with her soulful track `FU', a song that feels so grand in its delivery, almost serving up Broadway comparisons, and her vocals are tremendous here, especially in that chorus where she runs the gamut with her range, showing so much maturity in her voice yet remaining very fresh and young in her lyrical content.<br /><br />`Do My Thang' is the only point in the album where I'm a little apathetic.  The track is perfectly fine, but it feels `less than' the rest of the album, coming across like Britney Spears filler with edgier lyrics, but I'll give it that bass drop at the end of the last \\"Ima do my thang\\" because it's pretty cool.<br /><br />The album picks up with `Maybe Your Right', a track that pulls in the reigns and gives her a very straightforward country/pop sound and will attract the fans she already has, while showing a maturity and growth that feels more comfortable in mainstream/contemporary pop.  It's also a great way to wind down an album that prides itself on being brash.  Then she closes her album with `Someone Else', which takes a similar approach but without the country origins.  This is straight up pop, but with a reservation that isn't found on her more outlandish offerings.  It closes the album giving us the new Miley without the offensiveness and lets us all know that this girl can give us everything, and she truly has over the thirteen tracks we just witnessed.<br /><br />But wait, it's not over yet.<br /><br />Nope, if you got your hands on the Deluxe Album (I hope you have), then you are in for a treat.<br /><br />`Rooting for My Baby' has so much old soul it may take the listener by surprise.  If you told me that this was originally recorded by someone like Etta James, I'd believe you, it has that much languid beauty to it.  Miley's vocals are also very mature here.  She sounds like a girl twice her age, and I don't mean that in any degrading way.  She carries each note with a delicacy that defies her age and shows a control that most girls that young don't know.  `On My Own' is straight up 90's pop amazingness and gives me Michael Jackson chills and may be the best thing Miley has ever done.  With a rock edge and purely infectious vibe, I can't help but absolutely LOVE this song.  Then the album officially closes with the Ludacris laced club track, `Hands in the Air'.  It is a more restrained club track, made for the grind, and it works.  Miley owns it and Ludacris makes the most of his presence, and the mild musicality shifts are effective and give this a memorable feeling.<br /><br />My hands are up and all over this album!<br /><br />So there we have it, my complete breakdown of this album (I haven't done a review like this for an album in a while).  Say what you will about Miley's image now, but this girl is one smart cookie.  She knows how to sell herself, and she has given us exactly what we wanted.  She has hooked the world and the best thing about it is that she has actually delivered a brilliant album.  Had she not gotten our attention, can we really say we would have picked up the album?  Had we thought she was going to give us another `Party in the USA' would we have rushed out to snatch that plastic off `Bangerz'?  I don't think so; but girl cuts her hair, twirks some midgets and flashes a nipple and we flock like seagulls to the most surprisingly rewarding album of the year.delivery, almost serving up Broadway comparisons, and her vocals are tremendous here, especially in that chorus where she runs the gamut with her range, showing so much maturity in her voice yet remaining very fresh and young in her lyrical content.<br /><br />`Do My Thang' is the only point in the album where I'm a little apathetic.  The track is perfectly fine, but it feels `less than' the rest of the album, coming across like Britney Spears filler with edgier lyrics, but I'll give it that bass drop at the end of the last \\"Ima do my thang\\" because it's pretty cool.<br /><br />The album picks up with `Maybe Your Right', a track that pulls in the reigns and gives her a very straightforward country/pop sound and will attract the fans she already has, while showing a maturity and growth that feels more comfortable in mainstream/contemporary pop.  It's also a great way to wind down an album that prides itself on being brash.  Then she closes her album with `Someone Else', which takes a similar approach but without the country origins.  This is straight up pop, but with a reservation that isn't found on her more outlandish offerings. B002RI9M2U|4.0|Thirteen Reasons Why is a book about the suicide of a young high school girl names Hannah Baker, who recorded her reasons for committing the act on several old tapes. After her death, she sends the tapes to those she claims played a part in her decision. One of those involved is the main character, Clay, who listens to the tapes and adds his own thoughts to the narration.<br /><br />To start off, I really liked the concept the book was based upon. It was unique and I was definitely interested to hear all of the reasons for her death. I enjoyed how things played out for the main character and I agreed with a lot of Clay's input while he listens to the tapes. I also liked how the narrative makes it clear that the final decision was hers and that some of the blame she places on others is not entirely fair.<br /><br />Other than three events in the narration, though, there aren't really a lot of big reasons for why Hannah does what she does in the end. I guess I was expecting something a bit more traumatic, as many of the things that happened seem like the typical high school fare. However, I can also appreciate the perspective that it wasn't one big thing that culminated in her decision, but several little things that added up. It's also a good reminder that the way we treat other people matters.<br /><br />Overall, I enjoyed the story. I would recommend reading it as it was a quick read with a unique way of delving into a sensitive subject matter.

B00E7SXPVW|5.0|I'm going to start this off by saying I'm incredibly biased about this album. I  was born in 1990, and somehow,  by magic or fate,  went  fifteen entire years,  until 2005,  without the slightest bit of knowledge of who  nirvana were. (I was musically sheltered by my mother,  who listened only to oldies  and classical) sure,  I'dheard teen spirit  and lithium on the radio in that time,  but I'd never thought to ask the name of the band; I never cared.  until  the summer of 2005,  when I found this gem hiding in my father's cd  collection ( technically, I found it in the literal garbage he kept under his bed).  I consider myself lucky,  since I think there's no better she to discover nirvana  than 15.<br /><br />I found it,  and thought I had something special,  made just for me. I thought that I found something new, which I was determined to introduce my friends,  neigh,  the world to...  this is really embarrassing to admit,  given that this is an album  from one of the biggest bands of all time,  but my point is  that even without that context (I can't even describe how devastated I  was  when I  learned  Kurt Cobain  was  dead),  without even knowing who I was listening to,  this album blew my mind,  and changed my life.<br /><br /> now that story time's outta the way,  onto the review of the remaster...  I'm happy to say,  this is not nevermind. nevermind  it's old.  it sounds old.  there's no denying,  that album was made in the 90s.  the sound,  the production,  everything  about it reveals it's wrinkles.  it really needed a remaster,  and boy was that remaster...  disappointing.  no nuances were revealed;  if anything they were diminished,  hidden behind a wall of sound which was amped up to 11  for no reason  at all. a  remaster should not be louder,  it should be clearer.<br /><br /> my fear  was that they were going to do the same thing to in utero. I really didn't think this needed a remaster,  even. 20  years later,  this album sounds more fresh than some music that was made a  year ago. the last thing my life needed was some  record company exec bastardizing  one of the greatest things that had ever happened to me.<br /><br /> I'm happy to say the opposite occurred here from what happened with nevermind.  listening to the original side by side,  some sounds were actually  muffled,  just  shuttle bit ( not enough to take anything away)  in order to reveal little bits and pieces of sound I never knew existed ( even after hundreds of play throughs).  it's not louder,  it's clearer.  the original still sounds good after 20  years,  but this version  well still sounds great in a  hundred years.<br /><br /> top that of with the remastered  b-sides.  I already had mv  and I  hate myself,  but the versions I had...  left something to be  desired ( the version I  had of I  hate myself  and want to die,  which i'd  downloaded off of MySpace  in 2006,  sounded like an 8  track being played through a telephone).  it's nice to have em  both crystal clear for once.  once again, mv  has sent chills to my spine.  and the alt mixes  of  heart-shaped  box,  all apologies  and pennyroyal  tea are a real great listen.  heading what those songs could've been can make one really appreciate what they are.<br /><br /> disc 2  does the albini  mixes.  the differences a are both minute  and striking at the same time.  hardcore fans,  such as myself,  are sure to gobble up every removed  over dubbing,  every added piece of feedback  and studio noise,  while casual fans probably won't notice much of a difference. I love it,  because it sounds like nirvana were just playing the songs,  like they weren't in the studio;  like they were just playing a private,  intimate  concert for anyone willing to listen.  I'm sure some people will hate the removal of gloss from this already rough album.  still,  the extra lack of polish  adds an extra dimension  which allows one to experience this album like new  all over again,  and the extra feedback  mixed with the lack of over dubbing  probably makes this version of all apologies the most haunting available.<br /><br /> then there's the rio  demos... I  liven em, but  gonna leave them alone.  it's not required  listening,  but it's a nice extra.<br /><br /> To conclude, I am grateful for this release.  it added to the album,  painting a clearer picture of this rough masterpiece,  unlike it's predecessors  remaster.  the fact that some of the sounds were turned down rather than up really show the care  that was put into this,  adding yet more years  to this timeless masterpiece.  it brings instant happiness,  reigniteing  the dark,  yet whimsical wonder I had when I first experienced this music.  yet it also brings instant sorrow,  since there's nothing left to look forward to in the world of nirvana.  all I can do now is look back in awe. ( this cd was purchased in physical form  from a major retailer;  it was not downloaded or purchased via  Amazon) 5/5

B00DW1WR14|5.0|I love Pearl Jam. I have since they first hit the scene, and I've been lucky enough to see them live seven times over the past twenty years. I'm way too biased to even pretend I can give you an objective view, but I can offer up the following observations of &#34;Lightning Bolt&#34;:<br /><br /> The music on this album covers more range than I think I've ever heard from them. From their grungy roots to their emotional ballads to their experiments with jangly blues, new wave and psychedelia, everything is here.<br /><br /> At the same time, there is a cohesiveness to this album that I can't really recall on any Pearl Jam album since No Code. Whenever I start listening, I feel compelled to listen to the whole thing. That did not happen with Backspacer or the Avocado album. I think part of it is lyric-based. Everything on this album seems thematically connected, as there is an ever-present tension between inner emptiness and soul satisfaction. There is no Johnny Guitar or George Bush monologue to contend with. The stories that are told are more first-person and feel less contrived.<br /><br /> Ed is unafraid to let you into his heart, and that is a double-edged sword. If you buy into it and take his lyrics seriously, you will be richly rewarded. The band clearly is &#34;all in&#34; on Ed's style of songwriting at this point, and helps ensure that lyrics like the following, from &#34;Sirens,&#34; pack the necessary emotional punch:<br /><br />&#34;It's a fragile thing / This life we lead / If I think too much / I can get over- / whelmed by the grace / By which we live our lives / With death over our shoulders&#34;<br /><br />If you aren't looking for your heart strings to be pulled, and you have had a problem with Ed's soft side in the past, then that issue will likely come up again for you in spades with this album. On the other hand, you'll probably still dig the way he wails on songs like &#34;Infallible&#34; and &#34;Swallowed Whole,&#34; and &#34;Mind YourManners&#34; and &#34;Let the Records Play&#34; will probably appeal to the pit mosher and barn stomper in you.<br /><br /> There is a &#34;somber hopeful&#34; paradox that absolutely enthralls me. It goes hand in hand with what seems to be a new emphasis on setting the mood. After front-loading the album with three pretty straightforward songs of angst, Pearl Jam really starts revealing that mood at the song &#34;Sirens&#34;, which combines the dark imagery of ambulance sirens with transcendent lyrics of love. The music finds a way to echo this, which to me is somewhat of a revelation on this record. The new concert opener, &#34;Pendulum&#34; is also dark and beautiful. &#34;Swallowed Whole&#34; is an anthemic uplifting tune that has an unexpected, navel-gazing Pink Floyd keyboard drop in the middle. &#34;Yellow Moon&#34; has the kind of splintering tension that harkens back to &#34;Release&#34;. And while I originally thought &#34;Future Days&#34; was overly sappy, the dark church organ that booms during the &#34;I believe&#34; lyric keeps the tension tight in that song as well.<br /><br />All in all, I think this is one of Pearl Jam's finest works, exploring new ground while deftly weaving together the skills the band has acquired over the years. All the songs feel fresh and beg to be played live, and in my opinion, there isn't any &#34;filler&#34;, which cannot be said about every album by this band. Bravo!

B00DW1WR14|4.0|Even when Pearl Jam is down, they're never out.  The former grunge ragers saw their star dimming after a string of middling releases culminating in the early 2000s, but have been the beneficiaries of a full blown renaissance following the release of their 2006 self-titled album. In an age where many bands today rely on heavy helpings of studio trickery, Pearl Jam presents one of the most convincing arguments for pure guitar rock. For the most part, Lightning Bolt sticks to the blueprint established on 2009's Backspacer and the self-titled, but throws minor curveballs here and there.<br /><br />The lyrical topics sees Vedder keying in on questions of religion vs. science, God, nature, and his own mortality. Ravishing opener \\"Getaway\\" tackles these issues head on, with Vedder seemingly turning his back on religion as he declares that you sometimes must put faith in no faith. Jeff Ament's bass often finds itself buried under layers of guitars, but here it gets plenty of breathing room and provides the backbone for one of the album's crunchiest and most melodic rockers. \\"Mind Your Manners,\\" meanwhile, is a callback to the band's early grunge days with its blistering, grimy guitar and punk rock influence. With speed an intensity rivaling that of \\"Spin the Black Circle\\" from 1994's Vitalogy, it's sure to be a favorite with the headbangers.<br /><br />The concept of self-examination is another of Lightning Bolt's defining themes, and nowhere does this come across greater than on the striking ballad \\"Sirens.\\" This piece is custom designed to tug on your heartstrings, as Vedder reflects on his own mortality while hearing police sirens blare through the street late at night and ponders the ramifications of leaving his family behind. At times he raises into near falsetto, and sounds as though he's about to get teary eyed himself as he sings of dancing with laughter with the ever after. The theme of death and tombstones crop up more than once on Lightning Bolt, but never is theconcept painted in more dynamic brushstrokes than what we see here.<br /><br />Yet with all this discussion of Vedder, it's easy to get away from the varied instrumentation that makes up Lightning Bolt's colorful patchwork. Guitarists Mike McCready and Stone Gossard are once again in fine form. The title track features some of the band's tightest and most focused playing to date, presenting a passionate, stripped down, and immediate approach as waves of churning riffs and solos cascade out of your earbuds. \\"Mind Your Manners,\\" meanwhile, features a bridge that cuts through the muck and grime to deliver a sky high stanza, before giving way into a methodical and highly calculated solo.<br /><br />There are also some great moments of experimentation to be found dotting the album's second half. There is an ever so minor southwestern vibe that creeps up here and there, but is most pronounced on \\"Let the Records Play,\\" a grooving tune packed with smoky blues solos and a beat that could have been ripped directly from a ZZ Top song. It's not tough to imagine this playing at some backwater blues club frequented by the likes of Quentin Tarantino. Elsewhere, \\"Pendulum\\" develops at a slow, mellow pace and transfixes the listener's attention with a stunning sense of hypnotism. But it dissipates as quickly as it appeared, leaving you with a feeling of coldness and desolation.<br /><br />For all intents and purposes, Pearl Jam's 10th studio album is a fine achievement. The biggest drawback is that it often finds the band retreading similar musical territory they've been over numerous times. \\"Sleeping By Myself\\" and \\"Future Days,\\" aren't necessarily complaint worthy, but it sounds like the same type of low energy terrain we've covered before. It's good question as to whether the band will opt for a change of pace from here, but it's a question they can afford to put off for an album or two. Pearl Jam is still continuing to put out more than a handful of thought provoking hits, andthat should be more than enough for fans at the moment.

B00E5Q9VSW|3.0|A few years ago the Russian virtuoso Vadim Repin recorded the Brahms concerto with Chailly and the Leipzig Gewandhaus, and the hitch was with the orchestral part, where the conducting was correct but without warmth or passion and the orchestra - rebuilding under Chailly after decades of mediocrity in East Germany - was good but nothing more. Now the Greek virtuoso Leonidas Kavakos works under the same handicap. The timings have changed a bit, becoming slower in the first movement but faster in the finale. But what's missing can be heard by turning to Renaud Capucon's ravishing account with the vienna Phil. under Daniel Harding, where the essence is high romanticism all but drips form the score, and the orchestra sounds perfectly attuned to Brhams's idiom.<br /><br />To rise to such a high level, Kavakos would have to have something unusual or personal to say. Capucon made you feel that he believed in every bar of the music, which is absolutely demanded in a work this familiar. The soloist must breathe new life into it. In concert Kavakos gives off the same impression; he's a naturally warm, communicative performer, so it must be Chailly's coldness holding him back. The first movement is not memorable, but things improve in the Adagio, where Kavakos's playing is sweet and serenade-like rather than poignant and elegiac. Here the naturalness of his style speaks for itself; there's not a whiff of exaggerated feeling. He's quick and direct in the gypsy-flavored finale, leaving off the customary emphatic strokes that most soloists interject. But the unintended result, I'm afraid, is that the music sounds too offhand - we are meant to be dazzled, after all. Chailly is routine in both movements.<br /><br />Even though we live in a time of declining classical record sales, most of the big-name violinists have recorded the Brahms Cto., and I've listened and thought about the versions from Znaider, Tetzlaff, Repin, Capucon, and now Kavakos. I admire them all as musicians, but the cold-blooded side of me awards the palm, hands down to Lisa Batiashvili, a phenomenal player whose recording (on DG) leaps off the page with astonishing vibrancy; she is aided by remarkable conducting from Christian Thielemann leading the gorgeousu-sounding Dresden Staatskapelle.  I'd have to go back to Kremer/Bernstein, Oistrakh/Klemperer, and Heifetz/Reiner to find suitably remarkable rivals where the symphonic side of the Brahms concerto is rendered as magnificently as the solo part.<br /><br />The unusual fillers are the most welcome aspect of Kavakos's recording. Bartok'stwo Rhapsodies take a cue from the gypsy flavor of the finale of the Brahms. These are pieces that should strike directly to the emotional core, as they do when played by Bartok himself accompanying Josef Szigeti.  the other way to get the most out of them is in the ripe orchestral arrangements that are more commonly heard than the rather spare piano original - Stern and Bernstein give a big-hearted, throbbing account. Here, Kavakos and Nagy sound a bit sweet and tame, de-emphasizing the percussive, biting aspect of the writing.  They aren't very gutsy in the four Hungarian Dances of Brahms, delivered in the usual Joachim arrangements.<br /><br />Clearly Kavakos is following his instincts throughout, since everything is naturally musical, without showiness or exaggeration.  In that vein, he's very expert, but I'm not sure it's where the music really wants to go.  Gypsy and gentleman aren't two words that generally go together.the cold-blooded side of me awards the palm, hands down to Lisa Batiashvili, a phenomenal player whose recording (on DG) leaps off the page with astonishing vibrancy; she is aided by remarkable conducting from Christian Thielemann leading the gorgeousu-sounding Dresden Staatskapelle. B00EUS9JF0|3.0|Everything expressed here is under \\"IMHO\\" condition. So, please, try to be open-minded. If you still think that \\"Caesar's wife\\" cannot be under suspicion, let's just agree to disagree.  It's been more than 5 years since Paul's last album was released. So, I've been waiting for this one anxiously. Although I couldn't help but worry about the state of his voice: as has recently become evident (during the Jubilee concert alone), he is not being in his best vocal form nowadays. When \\"Kisses on the Bottom\\" came out, I bought it, listened to it and was immediately struck by how weak and old his voice sounded. I was not too eager to get on the bandwagon of those, who cried out that his voice was shot. I simply shrugged the worries of, having thought that it had to do with the genre untypical for him. But wait a minute, he has been writing and singing some immaculate \\"Granny\\" music for years. After several listens, I realized: I wish he'd done this project earlier... sometime in the 80s and 90s. It would have made sense at any point of his career, because he has been clearly showing affection for Tin Pan Alley since 1963 (yes, I am referring to his brilliant cover of \\"Till There Was You\\"). The record would have turned out much better, had he done it, when the voice was stronger. Then came the Nirvana re-union, where he sounded really confident, so my hopes were high again.    When I heard the \\"NEW\\" single, I was quite cheered up. I like the song and expected the album to be as good as \\"Memory Almost Full\\" in all aspects. Alas, having listened to the album for the first time, I was very much disappointed. I was in such disbelief that I decided to listen to the album for the second time, thinking I might have been in the wrong mood or something. While I liked and appreciated the songs better, I am still disappointed with the album. Why? As much as it hurts me to say this but... Paul McCartney completely lost his Beatle voice. There is hardly any semblance to it left.  Some people, even at a rather respectable age still have their voices intact: Jon Anderson and Tom Jones come immediately to mind. Paul, to my deepest regret, lost what used to be one of the most and yet most powerful voices in the history of world music. I know, I know... age is to blame for that. I am just amazed, why so many people fail to see this and all the critics. They destroyed him for Ram but praise him for this record? This is most confounding. Anyway, let's try to go track by track.    Save Us - the riff for some reason reminded of Wings. And immediately... vocals. His voice is hidden behind several filters and backing vocals. The riff is rather obnoxious... Keep on sending your love - is the best melodic moment in the song. I really don't understand why this one is the opening track instead of a much nicer \\"New\\", which would have been in tradition to the previous albums: just remember how many times Paul opened the album with the eponymous song.    Alligator - a much better track. The vocals... alas, they don't get any better. The falsetto part makes me cringe as well as some \\"modern\\" tricks, which sound dated for some reason, as if this part came from the 80s. Had the song been sung by somebody else, it would have been a nice little track since in terms of production, melody and hooks it's very much appealing to me.    On My Way To Work - a! The vocals are slightly better. Although this vibrato, which recently appeared in his voice is still there. Nice start to a cheery tune.  Interesting instrumental break, slightly seemed similar to Blur... I don't know, this instrumental break/riff sounds like something Albarn would do either in a late Blur or Gorillaz album... which is not a bad thing! In any case, the riff does surprise as it comes out of nowhere and is completely different mood-wise from the rest of the song.    Queenie Eye - the opening of this track is some of the most beautiful chords I have ever heard. My favourite moment of the album. This mellotrone opening could fit perfectly into any classical prog-rock album. Then the track takes a completely different direction, turning into a cheery pop tune. It is interesting in terms of melody and the chorus is also lovely. OUT Spells out reminded me of Christmas Time is Here Again. One of the more enjoyable tracks on the record. Although it also suffers from a falsetto interlude, which also possesses the typical muffled interlude, like they do in pop songs (all instrumentation is muted and muffled before coming back with gusto).    Early Days - Paul showcases his loveliest acoustic side again. Jesus... his voice sounds so old and tired. I almost cried listening to this. Not because the song touched me, but because I was imagining how the 5-6 year younger McCartney at least could sing it, and it just broke my heart. He goes again into falsetto.... Oh my.  The lyrics are nice though: autobiographic nature of them makes the song special. The song is beautiful if not sung by McCartney of today. The ooh-ooh chorus is reminiscent of \\"Mother's Nature Son\\", but only this time he simply cannot technically pull it off.    New - I liked it the first time I heard it on Paul's YouTube channel. A really nice track. Nice to see harpsichord back. It is both happy and sad. Classic McCartney - perhaps the best track on the album. Problems with voice are slightly less heard here thanks to dense production. Even the falsettos do not sound as bad.  The \\"I never know, what I can be, what I could do\\" reminded me of some song from a classic Disney cartoon... yes, it reminded me of Mrs. Potts' song from the sequence, when Beauty and the Beast play snowballs. Seems like the melodic line is rather similar. Very nice Brian Wilson tribute at the reprise of the song.    Appreciate - ah... Paul is trying to sound modern again. This sounds as awkward as \\"Ou Est Le Soleil\\". \\"Appreciate\\" refrain is strange and does not sound good in my opinion, makes the song repetitive. Again, the song itself is nice, but Paul trying to sound contemporary has just never worked for me. The best job he's ever done with trying to sound modern is just fooling around in the studio without any agenda in mind for McCartney II - such tracks like \\"Secret Friend\\" did sound contemporary and not forced. The best part of the song is the solo perchance played on that cigar box guitar of his... alas, it fades very quickly when the solo kicks in.    Everybody Out There - it is for some reason very similar to \\"Save Us\\". An average Wings song from 1976-1978 at best. The obvious sing-a-long part is just embarrassing. I can hear some slight similarity to \\"Young Boy\\". \\"Grace of God... and I\\" descending chorus is the best thing in the song. Sounds like a classic Paul melody right there albeit for a fraction of a second. And Paul pulls off polyphony quite nicely.    Hosanna - a great song, classic Paul, which means interesting melody and nice chord sequences. The vocals however... *sigh*... breaks my heart. He sounds so sad, as if he is about to cry because of that damned vibrato, which does not do him any favors. Sometimes he sounds like he is about to cry and I am sure the effect is unintentional. Love the closing instrumental fragment with the looped backwards riff.    I can Bet - intro reminds me of \\"Run, Run, Run\\" by The Who. Then kicks in a very nice classic Paul song. Very catchy and upbeat. Vocals are slightly masked by the filters, which make him sounds like Lennon on \\"Free as a Bird\\" demo with added reverb. Great solo in the middle too! One of the higher points of the album. Again, a very nice instrumental exit from the song.    Looking At Her - rather dated beat is what catches the eye... like a sore spot. I so wish Paul would stop trying sounding contemporary. A simple real beat by a real drummer and two guitars and a bass would have done the song more justice, which synths and electronic beats cannot do, making the sound of the song rather vulgar and \\"cheap\\" in my opinion. Melodically,the song is really good.    Road - brooding intro, nice piano driven riff after the chorus part. Paul vocally does sound better here, good enough not to spoil the musical experience... or am I just subconsciously trying to distance myself vehemently from the realization that Paul has lost his voice?    Turned Out - ah, I have high expectations for the bonuses. It is a known fact that sometimes Paul relegates some truly nice gems to B-sides/bonus tracks. This is no exception. Nice energetic intro, rather confident vocals and it's catchy too. I would make a substitution with this instead of either Save Us or Everybody Out There. Love the chorus. Could not get it out of my head. This track is surely one of the stronger cuts.    Get Me Out Of Here - okay, scratch \\"New\\". This! is my favourite track on the  entire album. Paul sings in such a way that his voice does not sound weak. Although the track does not have anything to do with BOTR song, it is still lovely. Great chords. It's simple and may sound like a pedestrian blues song, but I love it for some reason. \\"oh boy\\" sung in choir harks back to Travelling Willburys. I would not mind even if the song lasted longer. I truly enjoyed this song and it made me grin.    Scared - a nice piano ballad, which ends the album on a rather solemn note. One of the most sincere tracks on the album. Obviously, a love song inspired by his latest, thank God, happy relationship.    You've noticed perhaps, how many times I used a \\"classic Paul\\" expression. This is not coincidental: the five year break did him really a favour. The previous break from the solo career gave us \\"Flaming Pie\\", an album, which trumps many albums from the 80s or late 70s. The songs, vocals aside, are really good, make no mistake! Paul-composer has still got in him, beyond any doubt. His singing abilities have really suffered lately for whatever reason. Had he tried doing something, which would not demand a Beatle style vocal (like on \\"Get Me Out of Here\\") he wouldhave sounded much better. But since he is trying to sing like he used to for his entire life, he sounds just awful. It is just heart-breaking to hear his voice not spared by time. For some reason, it was very difficult to accept and I still can't. It just goes to show how soon wonderful things can happen... and if Paul's singing can cause such depressing thoughts in myself, a 22 year old, that is just sad. I had the same impression while listening to Sting's latest album, but since I care for Sting less, the effect was slightly less. Here, it is heart-wrenching. He can still write great songs, play like a virtuoso on multiple instrument, but! His voice is shot and this ruins the whole experience of the album for me. I was so bummed out that I quickly put \\"Chaos and Creation\\" afterwards and I was right: his voice sounds aged there, but he can still pull every vocal passage seemingly effortlessly. I remember MAF being slightly worse in this aspect, but still very much bearable. On \\"NEW\\", however, his voice has surely reached its nadir.    But hey, don't take me for my word. Who knows, maybe some of you might like his aged version. And every song on the album, but two, is wonderful, even when Paul is trying too hard to sound modern/contemporary. If not for his awfully shot voice, I would rank this record as highly as \\"Flaming Pie\\". With such a voice, or rather lack thereof, \\"NEW\\" is as good as \\"Dark Horse\\", when one is to take the vocal component into consideration. So... three stars: one for still exceptional composing skills, second one for \\"Get Me Out of Here\\" alone, and the third one... for old times' sake.  I still love Paul very much. He has done so much before he was 30, that he could release utter s*** for the rest of his life and still remain a legend. I am just happy to walk the Earth, while Paul McCartney is still creating new music. Definitely give the album a listen, but beware that it might disappoint considerably, like it did to me. As God is my witness... I really wanted to like this album... oh well, okay.

B00DW1WR14|5.0|Starting with a comment about production in no way diminishes the excellence of PJ's song craft on Lighting Bolt.  In these days when a &#34;good&#34; rock record has become synonymous with over-compression, O'Brien has helped PJ balance excellent songwriting with a truly organic sound.  Comparisons with the warm yet edgy sound of vinyl are hard to ignore here.  Close your eyes and turn your back on your CD player and you'll hear an analogue, not digital, disc.  If there's a band that can get the most out of O'Brien, it's Pearl Jam.  So throw away your surround sound, find a pair of stereo speakers, turn up the volume, and plant your head between them.  Play Lightning Bolt loud and let it strike.  Preferably again, again and again.  As far as the songs themselves, there is not a clunker in the bunch.  PJ is in fine form throughout.  Beginning with Getaway and culminating with Future Days, they've given it their all.  And it pays off in spades.  So far, nothing in 2013 even comes close.  So give it a listen.  You deserve it!

B00EY8ARYS|3.0|I don't honestly think that we can blame Joss Whedon personally for any of the problems that might exist with &#34;Marvel's Agents of S.H.I.E.L.D&#34;. This show bares the obvious trauma of a series that was &#34;grafted&#34; from a successful film enterprise into an attempted television series. And in that regard, Mr. Whedon was likely a short order cook taking direction from network and studio superiors. The show's concept is most clearly taken from the successful film outing &#34;Marvel's The Avengers&#34; (2012). The sheer number of superhero players in a 2-3 hour film enable you to concentrate on spectacle and interaction between the team members, and the audience was more than eager for that interaction between well known heroes like Iron Man, Thor, The Hulk, and of course recognized newcomers from the pre-existing solo films like agent Coulson and Nick Fury.  So . . . the team film could get away with the fact that the plot and action didn't permit much interaction with non-superhero types, whether they be family or significant loved ones.  NOW -- think about a team series that has to go the distance of 51 minutes a week for a 23-24 episode television series. Also,  &#34;Marvel's Agents of Shield&#34; is using the familiarity of Coulson and Fury to try to help us accept new recruits to S.H.I.E.L.D. 0307476464|2.0|On my scale, one star means I couldn't even finish it, two means I finished it but had to decide every time I picked it up whether or not to continue. Three is passable, say, for a new writer gaining experience.<br /><br />At its core, this book was a very imaginative tale. A girl somehow jumps to an alternate world, almost, but not quite the same as the one she (and you and I) was born in, figures it out, and (spoiler alert) finds her lost love and comes back to her own world time-line. On the way she runs into little people (not human little people but more like pixies that may or may not be malevolent), a religious cult out to get her, dodges a very resourceful and tenacious private eye, and finds out she's the second woman in history to get pregnant through immaculate conception. If someone, say Cole's notes, were to shorten this book to about a third of its length it would be a very interesting read. (Ironically, one of the main characters, Tengo, edits an unreadable manuscript, and keeping the author's voice makes it a best seller. This book very badly needed a Tengo to edit it.)<br /><br />Specific complaints:<br /><br />1. deus ex machnia all over the place. This book is in the general genre of an every-man put into a dangerous and potentially deadly situation, and using only his average ingenuity overcomes the bad guy and saves his life, all without the help of the author. In this book the author manipulates events by bringing in experts to save the character at moments crucial to the plot.<br /><br />2. Unbelievable uber-assassin-body-guard-good-guy. Think: James Bond. Ian Flemming didn't say \\"James Bond is a super-spy\\", he put Bond in tough situations, and by the way Bond gets out of them we conclude for ourselves that Bond is a super-spy. In this book, the author simply had this uber-assassin-body-guard-good-guy say \\"I'm an uber-assassin-body-guard-good-guy.\\" Lame.<br /><br />3. The dumbest uber-assassin-body-guard-good-guy I've every seen in a serious book. This guy is supposed to be super smart, and yet he lets a total amateur put herself in a dangerous situation, a situation that could have deadly consequences for the girl, for himself and his employer, and just says \\"OK.\\" Totally unbelievable for the character.<br /><br />4. Wasting my time on trivial explanations. Good writing either makes unusual situations usual, or makes usual situations unusual. Here the author spends pages and pages making usual situations mind-numbingly boring--and then repeats himself. Example: detailed descriptions going on for several pages of one of the characters making dinner. At first I thought there must be a key to understanding the character and read it carefully. Then the author did it again--and again--and again. Sometimes not about cooking, but all pointless time-wasters. By the end of the book I was just skipping them and as it turned out, they didn't contain any clues.<br /><br />5. All the characters were the same, just with different skins. A good writer has a voice for different characters, and each character will act and think differently in safe and dangerous situations. In this book all the characters, from hero to villain, and regardless of the situations, were incarnations of Aunt Bee sitting in her armchair knitting.<br /><br />If you're a real fan of the author, go ahead, but if not, buy something else.

1400052181|4.0|The story of the famous HeLa cell line, grown from Henrietta Lacks's cancer cells, is fascinating and rather surreal. How odd and bizarre that the same aggressive, dangerous cancer cells that killed Lacks at just 31 later went on to play such an important and vital role in medical research. The author explains in quite a bit of detail the medical significance of the HeLa line. I found this part of the book intriguing, but not emotionally stirring.<br /><br />But the story of Henrietta Lacks and her family? That was a more interesting tale, to me, than HeLa itself, and a highly disturbing one. As other reviewers have pointed out, the story of Henrietta and her family is in large part the story of poverty, racism, and the sad lack of opportunity that so many face in this supposed land of opportunity. To read of Henrietta herself: essentially orphaned at four years old, brought up in former slave quarters in the most primitive conditions, sexually exploited by her first cousin at 13, having her first child at 14, marrying that first cousin, enduring poverty and lack of consistent medical care, dying at 31 and leaving her own children to be essentially orphaned in turn? It's frankly a devastating story, however high-spirited and vivacious Henrietta evidently was in life.<br /><br />And then to read of her surviving children, whose present-day lives are also marred by poverty, ill health, lack of education, inadequate health care, and in some cases crime, is sad. When the author introduces us to the Lacks family, it is hard reading at first. Henrietta's children seem beset with hostility, superstition, suspicion of the larger society and the healthcare system in particular, anger, greed, and, most of all, a shocking level of ignorance. They hold any number of bizarre beliefs about HeLa--e.g., that Henrietta herself is somehow alive still, that her clones are walking the streets of London, that research done on the cells posthumously somehow physically hurt Henrietta herself.<br /><br />But as you go further, you realize the Lackses aren't stupid; they're just terribly misinformed and undereducated. Many of them don't even know what a cell is, much less DNA. When the author and a sympathetic researcher take the time to bring two of Henrietta's children to a laboratory  and actually show them HeLa cells under a microscope and in lab refrigerators and in clinical photographs and explain about the cells, the Lackses really begin to understand HeLa for the first time. Why didn't anyone take the time to really explain to them before?<br /><br />And toward the end of the book, you realize the Lackses' general suspicions of society and the healthcare system are not, in fact, founded on nothing. First, there is the issue of Henrietta's cancer cells being taken without full informed consent. This, while not right by today's standards, at least didn't physically harm anyone. But the healthcare system and society failed the Lacks family terribly in 1955, when Henrietta's oldest daughter Elsie died around age 16 at the Hospital for the Negro Insane in Crownsville, MD. It is likely that Elsie died from poor treatment, horrible unsanitary conditions, and, worst of all, painful experiments that were very likely performed on her and other patients at the hospital. So the Lackses' oldest daughter probably died directly from the negligence of the medical system.<br /><br />Elsie's story was the most profoundly moving part of this book. The tragedy of her short life and death disturbed me deeply. Elsie was committed to the hospital around age 11 with an unclear diagnosis. No one knows exactly what was wrong with her. Her death certificate says she had cerebral palsy. The family only recalls that she could not speak, was never toilet trained, and had seizures. But she could walk and run, and she knew who her mother was (and possibly others too). To think of this beautiful and helpless child being separated from her mother, probably her only source of comfort/safety, and being placed in an institution where she was hideously mistreated, shakes your faith in humanity and your assumptions that the Lacks family faced anything like the safety and opportunities that most of us expect in this country.<br /><br />This book raises important questions. It's very much worth reading. I give it four stars, instead of five, mainly because I found the author's presence in the book a little offputting. I don't know if there's any other way she could have told the story, but still I wish she could have minimized her own role in the events.

B005LAIGOQ|2.0|The Woman in Black stars Daniel Radcliffe (handling his own pretty well in his first post-Harry Potter role) as Arthur Kipps, a struggling lawyer in the United Kingdom's Edwardian era who lost his beloved wife during childbirth and has never quite gotten over it. Arthur is on the verge of being fired by his law firm but is given one last chance to keep his job. The assignment should be simple enough - just travel to far-away and remote Eel Marsh and handle the estate of its recently deceased owner. However, as you might expect, not long after Arthur arrives at the home, he starts hearing things, inanimate objects inexplicably start coming to life inside the house, and he sees a creepy woman in black roaming around the grounds. Oh, and did I mention that the children in the nearby town also start dying all around him at an alarming rate and it may be at the hands of the ghostly woman in black?<br /><br />It is very rare for modern mainstream horror movies to depict children dying or being murdered, and I applaud The Woman in Black for being so bold as to kill off young kids in some interesting and horrific ways. I cherish the 1980 classic The Children so much because it is one of the only horror movies I know of that goes balls to the wall and kills several little children in some of the goriest and most humorous ways imaginable. Don't worry, though, unlike the children in The Woman in Black, the kids in The Children were monstrous zombies and they had it coming. But I digress.<br /><br />Hardcore horror movie fans such as myself will not find The Woman in Black scary at all, but young teenagers and fans of Daniel Radcliff's Harry Potter may be sufficiently creeped out and find themselves jumping at a few points during this movie when the obligatory and garden variety non-threatening things such as birds, dogs, or townsmen jump out at our main character and make noise at the most inopportune moments. The theater my husband and I saw this movie in was packed to the gills with young kids and teenagers and I had to endure countless screams and awkward laughs during the \\"scary\\" parts of this movie.<br /><br />Hardcore horror movie fans will find a few things to enjoy in this movie, though. The film looks amazing and is beautifully shot, and the opening scene, involving a group of young girls who suddenly jump out of their attic window and plummet to their death, is haunting. The marsh, the woman in black, and the ghosts of her deceased victims also provide for a nice and eerie ambiance. It seems like more than a few movies released over the last year have employed a haunted house theme and the use of ghostly old women with evil intentions. Insidious did it flawlessly, while The Inn Keepers failed miserably. The Woman in Black, however, does a decent job in presenting a scary and ominous woman in black.<br /><br />You'll also love the wealthy landowner named Sam Daily (played by Ciar&aacute;n Hinds) and his eccentric and possessed wife (played by Janet McTeer) who befriend Arthur and inform him of the history of the town and the woman in black. There is one particular scene where Arthur has dinner with Mr. and Ms. Daily and learns of their son's tragic death that is both terrifying and hilarious at the same time, and I would have liked to have seen a lot more of this couple. I also would have liked it if the movie went on about 10 minutes longer than it did so we could have seen what became of the couple and their son who was taken from them too soon. I genuinely liked these people and would have liked for them to have found some closure or been reunited with the ghost of their long lost son. The movie would have been better if it had a little bit less of a confused Arthur walking up and down long hallways with a candle and a little bit more of the Daily family.<br /><br />This movie definitely isn't even close to perfect, and there are a few holes in the plot (e.g., Arthur's sidekick dog initially barks loudly at a few unseen ghosts in the house, but then completely disappears when the shit really starts to hit the fan, only to miraculously reappear in Arthur's car when it is time to flee the scene). However, overall The Woman in Black is a beautifully shot and well-acted little haunted house movie.he house, but then completely disappears when the shit really starts to hit the fan, only to miraculously reappear in Arthur's car when it is time to flee the scene).
